<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Joseph Priestley</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley.jpg"><img alt="Quarter-length portrait of a man in a black coat against a purple and blue curtain backdrop." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="905" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Priestley.jpg/250px-Priestley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Priestley.jpg/375px-Priestley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Priestley.jpg/500px-Priestley.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley by <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Sharples" title="Ellen Sharples">Ellen Sharples</a> (1794)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Joseph Priestley</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> 24 March [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 13 March] 1733 – 6 February 1804) was an 18th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Separatist" title="English Separatist">English Separatist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theologian" title="Theologian">theologian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemist</a>, innovative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguist" title="Linguist">grammarian</a>, multi-subject educator, and <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political theorist</a> who published over 150 works.  He has historically been credited with the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> having isolated it in its gaseous state, although <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> also have strong claims to the discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his lifetime, Priestley's considerable scientific reputation rested on his invention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_water" title="Soda water">soda water</a>, his writings on <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, and his discovery of several "airs" (gases), the most famous being what Priestley dubbed "dephlogisticated air" (oxygen). However, Priestley's determination to defend <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a> and to reject what would become the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_revolution" title="Chemical revolution">chemical revolution</a> eventually left him isolated within the scientific community.
</p><p>Priestley's science was integral to his theology, and he consistently tried to fuse <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> with Christian <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In his metaphysical texts, Priestley attempted to combine theism, <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a>, a project that has been called "audacious and original".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tap314_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tap314-6">[6]</a></sup> He believed that a proper understanding of the natural world would promote human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" title="Progress (history)">progress</a> and eventually bring about the Christian <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">millennium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tap314_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tap314-6">[6]</a></sup> Priestley, who strongly believed in the free and open exchange of ideas, advocated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">toleration</a> and equal rights for <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">religious Dissenters</a>, which also led him to help found <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianism</a> <a href="/wiki/History_of_Unitarianism#England" title="History of Unitarianism">in England</a>. The controversial nature of Priestley's publications, combined with his outspoken support of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, aroused public and governmental suspicion; he was eventually forced to flee in 1791, first to London and then to the United States, after a mob <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Riots" title="Priestley Riots">burned down</a> his Birmingham home and church. He spent his last ten years in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Northumberland County, Pennsylvania">Northumberland County, Pennsylvania</a>.
</p><p>A scholar and teacher throughout his life, Priestley also made significant contributions to <a href="/wiki/Pedagogy" title="Pedagogy">pedagogy</a>, including the publication of a seminal work on <a href="/wiki/Grammar#History" title="Grammar">English grammar</a> and books on history, and he prepared some of the most influential early timelines. These educational writings were among Priestley's most popular works. It was his metaphysical works, however, that had the most lasting influence, being considered primary sources for <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a> by philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>.
</p>

<h2><span id="Early_life_and_education_.281733.E2.80.9355.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education_(1733–55)">Early life and education (1733–55)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education (1733–55)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyBirthplace.jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white drawing of a two-story brick house along a road." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="484" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PriestleyBirthplace.jpg/220px-PriestleyBirthplace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PriestleyBirthplace.jpg/330px-PriestleyBirthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/PriestleyBirthplace.jpg/440px-PriestleyBirthplace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyBirthplace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley's birthplace (since demolished) in Fieldhead, <a href="/wiki/Birstall,_West_Yorkshire" title="Birstall, West Yorkshire">Birstall, West Yorkshire</a> – about six miles (10 km) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Priestley was born to an established English <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Dissenting</a> family (i.e. they did not conform to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Birstall,_West_Yorkshire" title="Birstall, West Yorkshire">Birstall</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Batley" title="Batley">Batley</a> in the <a href="/wiki/West_Riding_of_Yorkshire" title="West Riding of Yorkshire">West Riding of Yorkshire</a>. He was the oldest of six children born to Mary Swift and Jonas Priestley, a <a href="/wiki/Finishing_(textiles)" title="Finishing (textiles)">finisher</a> of cloth. To ease his mother's burdens, Priestley was sent to live with his grandfather around the age of one. He returned home, five years later, after his mother died. When his father remarried in 1741, Priestley went to live with his aunt and uncle, the wealthy and childless Sarah and John Keighley, 3 miles (4.8 km) from Fieldhead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odnb_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-8">[8]</a></sup> Because Priestley was precocious—at the age of four he could flawlessly recite all 107 questions and answers of the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Shorter_Catechism" title="Westminster Shorter Catechism">Westminster Shorter Catechism</a>—his aunt sought the best education for the boy, intending him for the ministry. During his youth, Priestley attended local schools where he learned Greek, Latin, and Hebrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1749, Priestley became seriously ill and believed he was dying. Raised as a devout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a>, he believed a <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">conversion experience</a> was necessary for salvation, but doubted he had had one. This emotional distress eventually led him to question his theological upbringing, causing him to reject <a href="/wiki/Unconditional_election" title="Unconditional election">election</a> and to accept <a href="/wiki/Universal_reconciliation" title="Universal reconciliation">universal salvation</a>. As a result, the elders of his home church, the <a href="/wiki/Independent_(religion)" title="Independent (religion)">Independent</a> Upper Chapel of <a href="/wiki/Heckmondwike" title="Heckmondwike">Heckmondwike</a>, refused him admission as a full member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-odnb_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's illness left him with a permanent stutter and he gave up any thoughts of entering the ministry at that time. In preparation for joining a relative in trade in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, he studied French, Italian, and German in addition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>, and Arabic. He was tutored by the Reverend George Haggerstone, who first introduced him to higher mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, logic, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> through the works of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Watts" title="Isaac Watts">Isaac Watts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willem_%27s_Gravesande" title="Willem 's Gravesande">Willem 's Gravesande</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daventry_Academy">Daventry Academy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Daventry Academy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Priestley eventually decided to return to his theological studies and, in 1752, matriculated at <a href="/wiki/Daventry_Academy" title="Daventry Academy">Daventry</a>, a Dissenting academy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Because he had already read widely, Priestley was allowed to skip the first two years of coursework. He continued his intense study; this, together with the liberal atmosphere of the school, shifted his theology further leftward and he became a <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters#Rational_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Rational Dissenter</a>. Abhorring dogma and religious mysticism, Rational Dissenters emphasised the rational analysis of the natural world and the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley later wrote that the book that influenced him the most, save the Bible, was <a href="/wiki/David_Hartley_(philosopher)" title="David Hartley (philosopher)">David Hartley's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Observations_on_Man" title="Observations on Man">Observations on Man</a></i> (1749). Hartley's psychological, philosophical, and theological treatise postulated a material <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">theory of mind</a>. Hartley aimed to construct a Christian philosophy in which both religious and moral "facts" could be scientifically proven, a goal that would occupy Priestley for his entire life. In his third year at Daventry, Priestley committed himself to the ministry, which he described as "the noblest of all professions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Needham_Market_and_Nantwich_.281755.E2.80.9361.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Needham_Market_and_Nantwich_(1755–61)">Needham Market and Nantwich (1755–61)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Needham Market and Nantwich (1755–61)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyGrammar.png"><img alt='Page reads: "The Rudiments of English Grammar; Adapted to the Use of Schools, with Observations on Style. By Joseph Priestley. London: Printed for R. Griffiths, in the Strand. M.DCC.LXI."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="661" data-file-width="352" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PriestleyGrammar.png/170px-PriestleyGrammar.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PriestleyGrammar.png/255px-PriestleyGrammar.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PriestleyGrammar.png/340px-PriestleyGrammar.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyGrammar.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rudiments_of_English_Grammar" title="The Rudiments of English Grammar">Rudiments of English Grammar</a></i> (1761)</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_education" title="Joseph Priestley and education">Joseph Priestley and education</a></div>
<p>Robert Schofield, Priestley's major modern biographer, describes his first "call" in 1755 to the Dissenting parish in <a href="/wiki/Needham_Market" title="Needham Market">Needham Market</a>, Suffolk, as a "mistake" for both Priestley and the congregation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Priestley yearned for urban life and theological debate, whereas Needham Market was a small, rural town with a congregation wedded to tradition. Attendance and donations dropped sharply when they discovered the extent of his <a href="/wiki/Heterodoxy" title="Heterodoxy">heterodoxy</a>. Although Priestley's aunt had promised her support if he became a minister, she refused any further assistance when she realised he was no longer a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a>. To earn extra money, Priestley proposed opening a school, but local families informed him that they would refuse to send their children. He also presented a series of scientific lectures titled "Use of the Globes" that was more successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2988" data-file-width="5312" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg/220px-Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg/330px-Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg/440px-Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sweetbriar_Hall_plaque.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sweetbriar Hall plaque</div></div></div>
<p>Priestley's Daventry friends helped him obtain another position and in 1758 he moved to <a href="/wiki/Nantwich" title="Nantwich">Nantwich</a>, Cheshire, living at <a href="/wiki/Sweetbriar_Hall" title="Sweetbriar Hall">Sweetbriar Hall</a> in the town's Hospital Street; his time there was happier. The congregation cared less about Priestley's heterodoxy and he successfully established a school. Unlike many schoolmasters of the time, Priestley taught his students <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> and even bought scientific instruments for them. Appalled at the quality of the available English grammar books, Priestley wrote his own: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rudiments_of_English_Grammar" title="The Rudiments of English Grammar">The Rudiments of English Grammar</a></i> (1761).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> His innovations in the description of English grammar, particularly his efforts to dissociate it from <a href="/wiki/Grammar#History" title="Grammar">Latin grammar</a>, led 20th-century scholars to describe him as "one of the great grammarians of his time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> After the publication of <i>Rudiments</i> and the success of Priestley's school, <a href="/wiki/Warrington_Academy" title="Warrington Academy">Warrington Academy</a> offered him a teaching position in 1761.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Warrington_Academy_.281761.E2.80.931767.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Warrington_Academy_(1761–1767)">Warrington Academy (1761–1767)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Warrington Academy (1761–1767)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5983" data-file-width="5092" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jasperware_cameo_portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_01.11.008.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jasperware cameo of Priestley,  Josiah Wedgwood &amp; Sons</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Mary.jpg"><img alt="Quarter-length portrait of a woman in a brown and grey lace bonnet adorned with a bow and leaning on her right hand." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="421" data-file-width="290" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Priestley_Mary.jpg/170px-Priestley_Mary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Priestley_Mary.jpg/255px-Priestley_Mary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Priestley_Mary.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Mary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mary Priestley, by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Frederik_von_Breda" title="Carl Frederik von Breda">Carl F. von Breda</a> (1793);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> daughter of <a href="/wiki/Ironmaster" title="Ironmaster">ironmaster</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Wilkinson" title="Isaac Wilkinson">Isaac Wilkinson</a>, sister of industrialist <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkinson_(industrialist)" title="John Wilkinson (industrialist)">John Wilkinson</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1761, Priestley moved to <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a> and assumed the post of tutor of modern languages and rhetoric at <a href="/wiki/Warrington_Academy" title="Warrington Academy">the town's Dissenting academy</a>, although he would have preferred to teach mathematics and natural philosophy. He fitted in well at Warrington, and made friends quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield-21">[21]</a></sup> These included <a href="/wiki/John_Aikin" title="John Aikin">John Aikin</a>, his sister <a href="/wiki/Anna_Laetitia_Barbauld" title="Anna Laetitia Barbauld">Anna Laetitia Aikin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood">Josiah Wedgwood</a>. Wedgwood met Priestley in 1762, after a fall from his horse. Wedgwood and Priestley met rarely, but exchanged letters, advice on chemistry, and laboratory equipment. Wedgwood eventually created a medallion of Priestley in cream-on-blue <a href="/wiki/Jasperware" title="Jasperware">jasperware</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distillations_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Distillations-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowden_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>37</span></sup>
</p><p>On 23 June 1762, Priestley married Mary Wilkinson of <a href="/wiki/Wrexham" title="Wrexham">Wrexham</a>. Of his marriage, Priestley wrote:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>This proved a very suitable and happy connexion, my wife being a woman of an excellent understanding, much improved by reading, of great fortitude and strength of mind, and of a temper in the highest degree affectionate and generous; feeling strongly for others, and little for herself. Also, greatly excelling in every thing relating to household affairs, she entirely relieved me of all concern of that kind, which allowed me to give all my time to the prosecution of my studies, and the other duties of my station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>On 17 April 1763, they had a daughter, whom they named Sarah after Priestley's aunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educator_and_historian">Educator and historian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Educator and historian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_education" title="Joseph Priestley and education">Joseph Priestley and education</a></div>
<p>All of the books Priestley published while at Warrington emphasised the study of history; Priestley considered it essential for worldly success as well as religious growth. He wrote histories of science and Christianity in an effort to reveal the progress of humanity and, paradoxically, the loss of a pure, "primitive Christianity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg"><img alt="A timeline, showing major civilisations" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1378" data-file-width="2201" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg/220px-A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg/330px-A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg/440px-A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_New_Chart_of_History.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A redacted version of <i><a href="/wiki/A_New_Chart_of_History" title="A New Chart of History">A New Chart of History</a></i> (1765); Priestley believed this chart would "impress" upon students "a just image of the rise, progress, extent, duration, and contemporary state of all the considerable empires that have ever existed in the world"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/Essay_on_a_Course_of_Liberal_Education_for_Civil_and_Active_Life" title="Essay on a Course of Liberal Education for Civil and Active Life">Essay on a Course of Liberal Education for Civil and Active Life</a></i> (1765),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_History_and_General_Policy" title="Lectures on History and General Policy">Lectures on History and General Policy</a></i> (1788), and other works, Priestley argued that the education of the young should anticipate their future practical needs. This principle of utility guided his unconventional curricular choices for Warrington's aspiring middle-class students. He recommended modern languages instead of classical languages and modern rather than ancient history. Priestley's lectures on history were particularly revolutionary; he narrated a <a href="/wiki/Providentialism" title="Providentialism">providentialist</a> and naturalist account of history, arguing that the study of history furthered the comprehension of God's natural laws. Furthermore, his <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">millennial</a> perspective was closely tied to his optimism regarding scientific progress and the improvement of humanity. He believed that each age would improve upon the previous and that the study of history allowed people to perceive and to advance this progress. Since the study of history was a moral imperative for Priestley, he also promoted the education of middle-class women, which was unusual at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Some scholars of education have described Priestley as the most important English writer on education between the 17th-century <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <i>Lectures on History</i> was well received and was employed by many educational institutions, such as New College at Hackney, <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yale" title="Yale">Yale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Priestley designed two <i>Charts</i> to serve as visual study aids for his  <i>Lectures</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> These charts are in fact timelines; they have been described as the most influential timelines published in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Both were popular for decades, and the trustees of Warrington were so impressed with Priestley's lectures and charts that they arranged for the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> to grant him a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Law</a> degree in 1764.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_electricity">History of electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History of electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly,_plate_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1658" data-file-width="1287" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg/220px-Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg/330px-Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg/440px-Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly%2C_plate_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Familiar_Introduction_to_Electricity_by_Joseph_Priestly,_plate_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley's "electrical machine for amateur experimentalists", illustrated in the first edition of his <i>Familiar Introduction to the Study of Electricity</i> (1768)</div></div></div>
<p>The intellectually stimulating atmosphere of Warrington, often called the "Athens of the North" (of England) during the 18th century, encouraged Priestley's growing interest in natural philosophy. He gave lectures on anatomy and performed experiments regarding temperature with another tutor at Warrington, his friend <a href="/wiki/John_Seddon_of_Warrington" title="John Seddon of Warrington">John Seddon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Despite Priestley's busy teaching schedule, he decided to write a history of electricity. Friends introduced him to the major experimenters in the field in Britain—<a href="/wiki/John_Canton" title="John Canton">John Canton</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Watson_(scientist)" title="William Watson (scientist)">William Watson</a>, and the visiting <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>—who encouraged Priestley to perform the experiments he wanted to include in his history. In the process of replicating others' experiments, Priestley became intrigued by unanswered questions and was prompted to undertake experiments of his own design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> (Impressed with his <i>Charts</i> and the manuscript of his history of electricity, Canton, Franklin, Watson, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Richard Price</a> nominated Priestley for a fellowship in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>; he was accepted in 1766.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1767, the 700-page <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_and_Present_State_of_Electricity" title="The History and Present State of Electricity">The History and Present State of Electricity</a></i> was published to positive reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The first half of the text is a history of the study of electricity to 1766; the second and more influential half is a description of contemporary theories about electricity and suggestions for future research. Priestley reported some of his own discoveries in the second section, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">conductivity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> and other substances and the continuum between conductors and non-conductors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S14456_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-S14456-39">[39]</a></sup> This discovery overturned what he described as "one of the earliest and universally received maxims of electricity", that only water and metals could conduct electricity. This and other experiments on the electrical properties of materials and on the electrical effects of chemical transformations demonstrated Priestley's early and ongoing interest in the relationship between chemical substances and electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Based on experiments with charged spheres, Priestley was among the first to propose that electrical force followed an <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> However, he did not generalise or elaborate on this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S14456_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-S14456-39">[39]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">general law</a> was enunciated by French physicist <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">Charles-Augustin de Coulomb</a> in the 1780s.
</p><p>Priestley's strength as a natural philosopher was qualitative rather than quantitative and his observation of "a current of real air" between two electrified points would later interest <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> as they investigated <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>. Priestley's text became the standard history of electricity for over a century; <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> (who later invented the battery), <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> (who discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> (who discovered <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>) all relied upon it. Priestley wrote a popular version of the <i>History of Electricity</i> for the general public titled <i>A Familiar Introduction to the Study of Electricity</i> (1768).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> He marketed the book with his brother Timothy, but unsuccessfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Leeds_.281767.E2.80.9373.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leeds_(1767–73)">Leeds (1767–73)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Leeds (1767–73)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyLeeds.jpg"><img alt="Half-length portrait of a man holding a small book in his right hand. He is wearing a black jacket and a white shirt." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="296" data-file-width="236" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/PriestleyLeeds.jpg/220px-PriestleyLeeds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/PriestleyLeeds.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyLeeds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The earliest known portrait of Priestley, known as the "Leeds" portrait (c. 1763); except for his membership on the Leeds Library Committee, Priestley was not active in the town's social life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps prompted by Mary Priestley's ill health, or financial problems, or a desire to prove himself to the community that had rejected him in his childhood, Priestley moved with his family from Warrington to <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a> in 1767, and he became <a href="/wiki/Mill_Hill_Chapel" title="Mill Hill Chapel">Mill Hill Chapel</a>'s minister. Two sons were born to the Priestleys in Leeds: Joseph junior on 24 July 1768 and <a href="/wiki/William_Priestley_(Louisiana_planter)" title="William Priestley (Louisiana planter)">William</a> three years later. <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Lindsey" title="Theophilus Lindsey">Theophilus Lindsey</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rector_(ecclesiastical)" title="Rector (ecclesiastical)">rector</a> at <a href="/wiki/Catterick,_North_Yorkshire" title="Catterick, North Yorkshire">Catterick, Yorkshire</a>, became one of Priestley's few friends in Leeds, of whom he wrote: "I never chose to publish any thing of moment relating to theology, without consulting him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Although Priestley had extended family living around Leeds, it does not appear that they communicated. Schofield conjectures that they considered him a <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Each year Priestley travelled to London to consult with his close friend and publisher, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Johnson_(publisher)" title="Joseph Johnson (publisher)">Joseph Johnson</a>, and to attend meetings of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_of_Mill_Hill_Chapel">Minister of Mill Hill Chapel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Minister of Mill Hill Chapel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_education" title="Joseph Priestley and education">Joseph Priestley and education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_Dissent" title="Joseph Priestley and Dissent">Joseph Priestley and Dissent</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyInstitutes.png"><img alt="Page reads: &quot;Institutes of Natural and Revealed Religion. In Two Volumes. Two which is prefixed, An Essay on the best Method of communicating religious Knowledge to the Members of Christian Societies. By Joseph Priestley, LL.D. F.R.S. The Second Edition. vol. I. Wisdom is the principal Thing. Solomon. Birmingham, Printed by Pearson and Rollason, for J. Johnson, No. 72, St. Paul's Church-Yard, London. M DCC LXXXIII.&quot;" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="438" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/PriestleyInstitutes.png/170px-PriestleyInstitutes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/PriestleyInstitutes.png/255px-PriestleyInstitutes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/PriestleyInstitutes.png/340px-PriestleyInstitutes.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyInstitutes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley had been working on <i><a href="/wiki/Institutes_of_Natural_and_Revealed_Religion" title="Institutes of Natural and Revealed Religion">Institutes of Natural and Revealed Religion</a></i> since his Daventry days.</div></div></div>
<p>When Priestley became its minister, <a href="/wiki/Mill_Hill_Chapel" title="Mill Hill Chapel">Mill Hill Chapel</a> was one of the oldest and most respected <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Dissenting congregations in England</a>; however, during the early 18th century the congregation had fractured along doctrinal lines, and was losing members to the charismatic <a href="/wiki/Methodism#Wesleyan_revival" title="Methodism">Methodist movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Priestley believed that by educating the young, he could strengthen the bonds of the congregation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his magisterial three-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Institutes_of_Natural_and_Revealed_Religion" title="Institutes of Natural and Revealed Religion">Institutes of Natural and Revealed Religion</a></i> (1772–74),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Priestley outlined his theories of religious instruction. More importantly, he laid out his belief in <a href="/wiki/Socinianism" title="Socinianism">Socinianism</a>. The doctrines he explicated would become the standards for <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a> in Britain. This work marked a change in Priestley's theological thinking that is critical to understanding his later writings—it paved the way for his <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Necessitarianism" title="Necessitarianism">necessitarianism</a> (the belief that a divine being acts in accordance with necessary metaphysical laws).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's major argument in the <i>Institutes</i> was that the only revealed religious truths that could be accepted were those that matched one's experience of the natural world. Because his views of religion were deeply tied to his understanding of nature, the text's <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a> rested on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argument_from_design" title="Argument from design">argument from design</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The <i>Institutes</i> shocked and appalled many readers, primarily because it challenged basic Christian orthodoxies, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">divinity of Christ</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">miracle of the Virgin Birth</a>. Methodists in Leeds penned a hymn asking God to "the Unitarian fiend expel / And chase his doctrine back to Hell."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Priestley wanted to return Christianity to its "primitive" or "pure" form by eliminating the "corruptions" which had accumulated over the centuries. The fourth part of the <i>Institutes</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_History_of_the_Corruptions_of_Christianity" title="An History of the Corruptions of Christianity">An History of the Corruptions of Christianity</a></i>, became so long that he was forced to issue it separately in 1782. Priestley believed that the <i>Corruptions</i> was "the most valuable" work he ever published. In demanding that his readers apply the logic of the emerging sciences and comparative history to the Bible and Christianity, he alienated religious and scientific readers alike—scientific readers did not appreciate seeing science used in the defence of religion and religious readers dismissed the application of science to religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_controversialist">Religious controversialist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Religious controversialist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Priestley engaged in numerous political and religious <a href="/wiki/Pamphlet" title="Pamphlet">pamphlet</a> wars. According to Schofield, "he entered each controversy with a cheerful conviction that he was right, while most of his opponents were convinced, from the outset, that he was willfully and maliciously wrong. He was able, then, to contrast his sweet reasonableness to their personal rancor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S181_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-S181-56">[56]</a></sup> but as Schofield points out Priestley rarely altered his opinion as a result of these debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S181_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-S181-56">[56]</a></sup> While at Leeds he wrote controversial pamphlets on the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Lord's Supper</a> and on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> doctrine; thousands of copies were published, making them some of Priestley's most widely read works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley founded the <i><a href="/wiki/Theological_Repository" title="Theological Repository">Theological Repository</a></i> in 1768, a journal committed to the open and rational inquiry of theological questions. Although he promised to print any contribution, only like-minded authors submitted articles. He was therefore obliged to provide much of the journal's content himself (this material became the basis for many of his later theological and metaphysical works). After only a few years, due to a lack of funds, he was forced to cease publishing the journal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> He revived it in 1784 with similar results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defender_of_Dissenters_and_political_philosopher">Defender of Dissenters and political philosopher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Defender of Dissenters and political philosopher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_Dissent" title="Joseph Priestley and Dissent">Joseph Priestley and Dissent</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg"><img alt="Page reads: &quot;An Essay on the First Principles of Government, and on the Nature of Political, Civil, and Religious Liberty, including Remarks on Dr. Brown's Code of Education, and on Br. Balguy's Sermon on Church Authority. The Second Edition, corrected and enlarged, by Joseph Priestley, LL.D. F.R.S. London: Printed for J. Dodsley, in Pall-Mall; T. Cadell, (successor to Mr. Millar) in the Strand; and J. Johnson, No. 72 in St. Paul's Church-Yard. MDCCLXXI.&quot;" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="345" height="435" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg/220px-PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg/330px-PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyFirstPrinciples.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>His <i><a href="/wiki/Essay_on_the_First_Principles_of_Government" title="Essay on the First Principles of Government">Essay on the First Principles of Government</a></i> (1768) influenced early 19th-century political philosophers, including <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Many of Priestley's political writings supported the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corporation_Act_1661" title="Corporation Act 1661">Corporation Acts</a>, which restricted the rights of Dissenters. They could not hold political office, serve in the armed forces, or attend Oxford and Cambridge unless they subscribed to the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-nine_Articles" title="Thirty-nine Articles">Thirty-nine Articles</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Dissenters repeatedly petitioned Parliament to repeal the Acts, arguing that they were being treated as second-class citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's friends, particularly other Rational Dissenters, urged him to publish a work on the injustices experienced by Dissenters; the result was his <i><a href="/wiki/Essay_on_the_First_Principles_of_Government" title="Essay on the First Principles of Government">Essay on the First Principles of Government</a></i> (1768).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> An early work of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">modern liberal political theory</a> and Priestley's most thorough treatment of the subject, it—unusually for the time—distinguished political rights from civil rights with precision and argued for expansive civil rights. Priestley identified separate private and public spheres, contending that the government should only have control over the public sphere. Education and religion, in particular, he maintained, were matters of private conscience and should not be administered by the state. Priestley's later radicalism emerged from his belief that the British government was infringing upon these individual freedoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley also defended the rights of Dissenters against the attacks of <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a>, an eminent legal theorist, whose <i><a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_the_Laws_of_England" title="Commentaries on the Laws of England">Commentaries on the Laws of England</a></i> (1765–69) had become the standard legal guide. Blackstone's book stated that dissent from the Church of England was a crime and that Dissenters could not be loyal subjects. Furious, Priestley lashed out with his <i>Remarks on Dr. Blackstone's Commentaries</i> (1769), correcting Blackstone's interpretation of the law, his grammar (a highly politicised subject at the time), and history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Blackstone, chastened, altered subsequent editions of his <i>Commentaries</i>: he rephrased the offending passages and removed the sections claiming that Dissenters could not be loyal subjects, but he retained his description of Dissent as a crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Natural_philosopher:_electricity.2C_Optics.2C_and_soda_water"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_philosopher:_electricity,_Optics,_and_soda_water">Natural philosopher: electricity, <i>Optics</i>, and soda water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural philosopher: electricity, Optics, and soda water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Priestley%27s_book_Optics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="108" data-file-width="227" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Joseph_Priestley%27s_book_Optics.jpg/220px-Joseph_Priestley%27s_book_Optics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Joseph_Priestley%27s_book_Optics.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Priestley%27s_book_Optics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Optics: The History and Present State of Vision, Light, and Colours</i>, published in 1772, London</div></div></div>
<p>Although Priestley claimed that <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> was only a hobby, he took it seriously. In his <i>History of Electricity</i>, he described the scientist as promoting the "security and happiness of mankind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Priestley's science was eminently practical and he rarely concerned himself with theoretical questions; his model was <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>. When he moved to Leeds, Priestley continued his electrical and chemical experiments (the latter aided by a steady supply of carbon dioxide from a neighbouring brewery). Between 1767 and 1770, he presented five papers to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> from these initial experiments; the first four papers explored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronal_discharge" title="Coronal discharge">coronal discharges</a> and other phenomena related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_discharge" title="Electrical discharge">electrical discharge</a>, while the fifth reported on the conductivity of charcoals from different sources. His subsequent experimental work focused on chemistry and <a href="/wiki/Pneumatics" title="Pneumatics">pneumatics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley published the first volume of his projected history of experimental philosophy, <i>The History and Present State of Discoveries Relating to Vision, Light and Colours</i> (referred to as his <i>Optics</i>), in 1772.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> He paid careful attention to the history of optics and presented excellent explanations of early optics experiments, but his mathematical deficiencies caused him to dismiss several important contemporary theories. Furthermore, he did not include any of the practical sections that had made his <i>History of Electricity</i> so useful to practising natural philosophers. Unlike his <i>History of Electricity</i>, it was not popular and had only one edition, although it was the only English book on the topic for 150 years. The hastily written text sold poorly; the cost of researching, writing, and publishing the <i>Optics</i> convinced Priestley to abandon his history of experimental philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><caption>External audio</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.sciencehistory.org/distillations/podcast/fizzy-water" rel="nofollow">“Fizzy Water”</a>, <i>Distillations</i> Podcast Episode 217, <a href="/wiki/Science_History_Institute" title="Science History Institute">Science History Institute</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Priestley was considered for the position of astronomer on <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>'s <a href="/wiki/James_Cook#Second_voyage_(1772–75)" title="James Cook">second voyage to the South Seas</a>, but was not chosen. Still, he contributed in a small way to the voyage: he provided the crew with a method for making <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">soda water</a>, which he erroneously speculated might be a cure for <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>. He then published a pamphlet with <i>Directions for Impregnating Water with Fixed Air</i> (1772).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Priestley did not exploit the commercial potential of soda water, but others such as <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Johann_Jacob_Schweppe" title="Johann Jacob Schweppe">J. J. Schweppe</a></span> made fortunes from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In 1773, the Royal Society recognised Priestley's achievements in natural philosophy by awarding him the <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Medal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's friends wanted to find him a more financially secure position. In 1772, prompted by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Richard Price</a> and Benjamin Franklin, <a href="/wiki/William_Petty,_2nd_Earl_of_Shelburne" title="William Petty, 2nd Earl of Shelburne">Lord Shelburne</a> wrote to Priestley asking him to direct the education of his children and to act as his general assistant. Although Priestley was reluctant to sacrifice his ministry, he accepted the position, resigning from Mill Hill Chapel on 20 December 1772, and preaching his last sermon on 16 May 1773.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Calne_.281773.E2.80.9380.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Calne_(1773–80)">Calne (1773–80)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Calne (1773–80)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg"><img alt="Portrait of a man sitting on a chair and leaning against a table with books and papers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="472" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Joseph_Priestley_by_Henry_Fuseli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait of Priestley commissioned by his publisher and close friend <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Johnson_(publisher)" title="Joseph Johnson (publisher)">Joseph Johnson</a> from <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fuseli" title="Henry Fuseli">Henry Fuseli</a> (c. 1783)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1773, the Priestleys moved to <a href="/wiki/Calne" title="Calne">Calne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a>, and a year later <a href="/wiki/William_Petty,_2nd_Earl_of_Shelburne" title="William Petty, 2nd Earl of Shelburne">Lord Shelburne</a> and Priestley took a tour of Europe. According to Priestley's close friend <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Lindsey" title="Theophilus Lindsey">Theophilus Lindsey</a>, Priestley was "much improved by this view of mankind at large".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Upon their return, Priestley easily fulfilled his duties as librarian and tutor. The workload was intentionally light, allowing him time to pursue his scientific investigations and theological interests. Priestley also became a political adviser to Shelburne, gathering information on parliamentary issues and serving as a liaison between Shelburne and the Dissenting and American interests. When the Priestleys' third son was born on 24 May 1777, they named him Henry at the lord's request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materialist_philosopher">Materialist philosopher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Materialist philosopher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_Dissent" title="Joseph Priestley and Dissent">Joseph Priestley and Dissent</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyMatterSpirit.png"><img alt='Page reads: "Disquisitions relating to Matter and Spirit. To which is added, The History of the Philosophical Doctrine concerning the Origin of the Soul, and the Nature of Matter; with its Influence on Christianity, especially with Respect to the Doctrine of the Pre-existence of Christ."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="282" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PriestleyMatterSpirit.png/170px-PriestleyMatterSpirit.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PriestleyMatterSpirit.png/255px-PriestleyMatterSpirit.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/PriestleyMatterSpirit.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyMatterSpirit.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>By 1782, at least a dozen hostile refutations were published to <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitions_relating_to_Matter_and_Spirit" title="Disquisitions relating to Matter and Spirit">Disquisitions relating to Matter and Spirit</a></i>, and Priestley was branded an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">atheist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Priestley wrote his most important philosophical works during his years with Lord Shelburne. In a series of major <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> texts published between 1774 and 1780—<i>An Examination of Dr. Reid's Inquiry into the Human Mind</i> (1774), <i>Hartley's Theory of the Human Mind on the Principle of the Association of Ideas</i> (1775), <i><a href="/wiki/Disquisitions_relating_to_Matter_and_Spirit" title="Disquisitions relating to Matter and Spirit">Disquisitions relating to Matter and Spirit</a></i> (1777), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Doctrine_of_Philosophical_Necessity_Illustrated" title="The Doctrine of Philosophical Necessity Illustrated">The Doctrine of Philosophical Necessity Illustrated</a></i> (1777), and <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_to_a_Philosophical_Unbeliever" title="Letters to a Philosophical Unbeliever">Letters to a Philosophical Unbeliever</a></i> (1780)—he argues for a philosophy that incorporates four concepts: <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Necessitarianism" title="Necessitarianism">necessitarianism</a>. By studying the natural world, he argued, people would learn how to become more compassionate, happy, and prosperous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley strongly suggested that there is no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">mind-body duality</a>, and put forth a materialist philosophy in these works; that is, one founded on the principle that everything in the universe is made of matter that we can perceive. He also contended that discussing the soul is impossible because it is made of a divine substance, and humanity cannot perceive the divine. Despite his separation of the divine from the mortal, this position shocked and angered many of his readers, who believed that such a duality was necessary for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a> to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Responding to <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">Baron d'Holbach</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_System_of_Nature" title="The System of Nature">Système de la Nature</a></i> (1770) and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogues_Concerning_Natural_Religion" title="Dialogues Concerning Natural Religion">Dialogues Concerning Natural Religion</a></i> (1779) as well as the works of the French <i>philosophers</i>, Priestley maintained that materialism and determinism could be reconciled with a belief in God. He criticised those whose faith was shaped by books and fashion, drawing an analogy between the scepticism of educated men and the credulity of the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maintaining that humans had no <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, Priestley argued that what he called "philosophical necessity" (akin to <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">absolute determinism</a>) is consonant with Christianity, a position based on his understanding of the natural world. Like the rest of nature, man's mind is subject to the laws of causation, Priestley contended, but because a benevolent God created these laws, the world and the people in it will eventually be perfected. Evil is therefore only an imperfect understanding of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Priestley's philosophical work has been characterised as "audacious and original",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tap314_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tap314-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> it partakes of older philosophical traditions on the problems of <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM341_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-MM341-83">[83]</a></sup> For example, the 17th-century philosopher <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> argued for absolute determinism and absolute materialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Like Spinoza<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and Priestley,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a> argued that human will was completely determined by natural laws;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_1998_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams_1998-87">[87]</a></sup>
however, unlike them, Leibniz argued for a "parallel universe" of immaterial objects (such as human souls) so arranged by God that its outcomes agree exactly with those of the material universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
Leibniz<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
and Priestley<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
share an optimism that God has chosen the chain of events benevolently; however, Priestley believed that the events were leading to a glorious <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">Millennial</a> conclusion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tap314_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tap314-6">[6]</a></sup> whereas for Leibniz the entire chain of events was optimal in and of itself, as compared with other conceivable chains of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founder_of_Unitarianism">Founder of Unitarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Founder of Unitarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Unitarianism" title="History of Unitarianism">History of Unitarianism</a></div>
<p>When Priestley's friend <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Lindsey" title="Theophilus Lindsey">Theophilus Lindsey</a> decided to found a new Christian denomination that would not restrict its members' beliefs, Priestley and others hurried to his aid. On 17 April 1774, Lindsey held the first <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> service in Britain, at the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Essex_Street_Chapel" title="Essex Street Chapel">Essex Street Chapel</a> in London; he had even designed his own liturgy, of which many were critical. Priestley defended his friend in the pamphlet <i>Letter to a Layman, on the Subject of the Rev. Mr. Lindsey's Proposal for a Reformed English Church</i> (1774),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> claiming that only the form of worship had been altered, not its substance, and attacking those who followed religion as a fashion. Priestley attended Lindsey's church regularly in the 1770s and occasionally preached there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> He continued to support institutionalised Unitarianism for the rest of his life, writing several <i>Defenses</i> of Unitarianism and encouraging the foundation of new Unitarian chapels throughout Britain and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experiments_and_Observations_on_Different_Kinds_of_Air">Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/An_Inventory_of_the_Furniture_in_Dr._Priestley%27s_Study" title="wikisource:An Inventory of the Furniture in Dr. Priestley's Study">Wikisource:An Inventory of the Furniture in Dr. Priestley's Study</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg"><img alt="Engraving of assorted scientific equipment, such as a pneumatic trough. A dead mouse rests under one glass canister." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="875" data-file-width="1063" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg/350px-Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg/525px-Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg/700px-Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Joseph_pneumatic_trough.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Equipment used by Priestley in his experiments on gases, 1775</div></div></div>
<p>Priestley's years in Calne were the only ones in his life dominated by scientific investigations; they were also the most scientifically fruitful. His experiments were almost entirely confined to "airs", and out of this work emerged his most important scientific texts: the six volumes of <i><a href="/wiki/Experiments_and_Observations_on_Different_Kinds_of_Air" title="Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air">Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air</a></i> (1774–86).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> These experiments helped repudiate the last vestiges of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">theory of four elements</a>, which Priestley attempted to replace with his own variation of <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a>. According to that 18th-century theory, the combustion or <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation</a> of a substance corresponded to the release of a material substance, <i>phlogiston</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's work on "airs" is not easily classified. As historian of science <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schaffer" title="Simon Schaffer">Simon Schaffer</a> writes, it "has been seen as a branch of physics, or chemistry, or natural philosophy, or some highly idiosyncratic version of Priestley's own invention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Furthermore, the volumes were both a scientific and a political enterprise for Priestley, in which he argues that science could destroy "undue and usurped authority" and that government has "reason to tremble even at an air pump or an electrical machine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Volume I of <i>Experiments and Observations on Different Kinds of Air</i> outlined several discoveries: "nitrous air" (<a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide" title="Nitric oxide">nitric oxide</a>, NO); "vapor of spirit of salt", later called "acid air" or "marine acid air" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhydrous_hydrochloric_acid" title="Anhydrous hydrochloric acid">anhydrous hydrochloric acid</a>, HCl); "alkaline air" (<a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, NH<sub>3</sub>); "diminished" or "dephlogisticated nitrous air" (<a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>, N<sub>2</sub>O); and, most famously, "dephlogisticated air" (<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, O<sub>2</sub>) as well as experimental findings that showed plants revitalised enclosed volumes of air, a discovery that would eventually lead to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Priestley also developed a "nitrous air test" to determine the "goodness of air". Using a <a href="/wiki/Pneumatic_trough" title="Pneumatic trough">pneumatic trough</a>, he would mix nitrous air with a test sample, over water or mercury, and measure the decrease in volume—the principle of <a href="/wiki/Eudiometer" title="Eudiometer">eudiometry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fruton_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fruton-100">[100]</a></sup> After a small history of the study of airs, he explained his own experiments in an open and sincere style. As an early biographer writes, "whatever he knows or thinks he tells: doubts, perplexities, blunders are set down with the most refreshing candour."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Priestley also described his cheap and easy-to-assemble experimental apparatus; his colleagues therefore believed that they could easily reproduce his experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Faced with inconsistent experimental results, Priestley employed phlogiston theory. This, however, led him to conclude that there were only three types of "air": "fixed", "alkaline", and "acid". Priestley dismissed the <a href="/wiki/History_of_chemistry#17th_and_18th_centuries:_Early_chemistry" title="History of chemistry">burgeoning chemistry</a> of his day. Instead, he focused on gases and "changes in their sensible properties", as had natural philosophers before him. He isolated <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO), but apparently did not realise that it was a separate "air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
