<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_oxygen">Discovery of oxygen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Discovery of oxygen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Mouse%27s_Petition" title="wikisource:The Mouse's Petition">Wikisource:The Mouse's Petition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a laboratory, with glass-encased, wooden bookcases on two walls and a window on the third. There is a display case in the middle of the room." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg/220px-Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg/330px-Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg/440px-Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bowood_House_laboratory.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The laboratory at Lord Shelburne's estate, <a href="/wiki/Bowood_House" title="Bowood House">Bowood House</a>, in which Priestley discovered oxygen</div></div></div>
<p>In August 1774 he isolated an "air" that appeared to be completely new, but he did not have an opportunity to pursue the matter because he was about to tour Europe with Shelburne. While in Paris, however, Priestley managed to replicate the experiment for others, including French chemist <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a>. After returning to Britain in January 1775, he continued his experiments and discovered "vitriolic acid air" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur_dioxide" title="Sulphur dioxide">sulphur dioxide</a>, SO<sub>2</sub>).
</p><p>In March he wrote to several people regarding the new "air" that he had discovered in August. One of these letters was read aloud to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, and a paper outlining the discovery, titled "An Account of further Discoveries in Air", was published in the Society's journal <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions" title="Philosophical Transactions">Philosophical Transactions</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Priestley called the new substance "dephlogisticated air", which he made in the famous experiment by <a href="/wiki/Burning_glass" title="Burning glass">focusing the sun’s rays</a> on a sample of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercuric_oxide" title="Mercuric oxide">mercuric oxide</a>. He first tested it on mice, who surprised him by surviving quite a while entrapped with the air, and then on himself, writing that it was "five or six times better than common air for the purpose of respiration, inflammation, and, I believe, every other use of common atmospherical air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> He had discovered <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> gas (O<sub>2</sub>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shelburne.jpg"><img alt="Half-length portrait of a man wearing furred robes and a white wig and looking regal. Underneath his white robes, he is wearing red and gold and he is sitting in a red chair." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="830" data-file-width="716" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Shelburne.jpg/220px-Shelburne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Shelburne.jpg/330px-Shelburne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Shelburne.jpg/440px-Shelburne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shelburne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Petty,_2nd_Earl_of_Shelburne" title="William Petty, 2nd Earl of Shelburne">William Petty, 2nd Earl of Shelburne</a> – a fellow Unitarian – built a laboratory for the famous dissenter at <a href="/wiki/Bowood_House" title="Bowood House">Bowood House</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Priestley assembled his oxygen paper and several others into a second volume of <i>Experiments and Observations on Air</i>, published in 1776. He did not emphasise his discovery of "dephlogisticated air" (leaving it to Part III of the volume) but instead argued in the preface how important such discoveries were to rational religion. His paper narrated the discovery chronologically, relating the long delays between experiments and his initial puzzlements; thus, it is difficult to determine when exactly Priestley "discovered" oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Such dating is significant as both Lavoisier and Swedish pharmacist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> have strong claims to the discovery of oxygen as well, Scheele having been the first to isolate the gas (although he published after Priestley) and Lavoisier having been the first to describe it as purified "air itself entire without alteration" (that is, the first to explain oxygen without phlogiston theory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his paper "Observations on Respiration and the Use of the Blood", Priestley was the first to suggest a connection between blood and air, although he did so using <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a>. In typical Priestley fashion, he prefaced the paper with a history of the study of respiration. A year later, clearly influenced by Priestley, Lavoisier was also discussing respiration at the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">Académie des sciences</a>. Lavoisier's work began the long train of discovery that produced papers on oxygen respiration and culminated in the overthrow of phlogiston theory and the establishment of modern chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1779 Priestley and Shelburne had a rupture, the precise reasons for which remain unclear. Shelburne blamed Priestley's health, while Priestley claimed Shelburne had no further use for him. Some contemporaries speculated that Priestley's outspokenness had hurt Shelburne's political career. Schofield argues that the most likely reason was Shelburne's recent marriage to Louisa Fitzpatrick—apparently, she did not like the Priestleys. Although Priestley considered moving to America, he eventually accepted <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> New Meeting's offer to be their minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Priestley and Shelburne's families upheld their Unitarian faith  for generations.
In December 2013, it was reported that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Christopher_Bullock" title="Sir Christopher Bullock">Sir Christopher Bullock</a> – a direct descendant of Shelburne's brother, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Fitzmaurice_(MP)" title="Thomas Fitzmaurice (MP)">Thomas Fitzmaurice (MP)</a> – had married his wife, <a href="/wiki/Lupton_family" title="Lupton family">Lady Bullock</a>, née Barbara May Lupton, at London's Unitarian <a href="/wiki/Essex_Street_Chapel" title="Essex Street Chapel">Essex Church</a> in 1917. Barbara Lupton was the second cousin of <a href="/wiki/Family_of_Catherine,_Duchess_of_Cambridge" title="Family of Catherine, Duchess of Cambridge">Olive Middleton</a>, née Lupton, the great grandmother of <a href="/wiki/Catherine,_Duchess_of_Cambridge" title="Catherine, Duchess of Cambridge">Catherine, Duchess of Cambridge</a>. In 1914, Olive and Noel Middleton had married at Leeds' <a href="/wiki/Mill_Hill_Chapel" title="Mill Hill Chapel">Mill Hill Chapel</a>, which Priestly, as its minister, had once guided towards Unitarianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Birmingham_.281780.E2.80.9391.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Birmingham_(1780–91)">Birmingham (1780–91)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Birmingham (1780–91)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1780 the Priestleys moved to <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and spent a happy decade surrounded by old friends, until they were forced to flee in 1791 by religiously motivated mob violence in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Riots" title="Priestley Riots">Priestley Riots</a>. Priestley accepted the ministerial position at New Meeting on the condition that he be required to preach and teach only on Sundays, so that he would have time for his writing and scientific experiments. As in Leeds, Priestley established classes for the youth of his parish and by 1781, he was teaching 150 students. Because Priestley's New Meeting salary was only 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" title="Guinea (British coin)">guineas</a>, friends and patrons donated money and goods to help continue his investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> He was elected a Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1782.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_Revolution">Chemical Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chemical Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_Revolution" title="Chemical Revolution">Chemical Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg/170px-Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg/255px-Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg/340px-Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Society_Moonstones_22_Priestley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of a set of <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Society_Moonstones" title="Lunar Society Moonstones">Lunar Society Moonstones</a> commemorating Priestley at <a href="/wiki/Great_Barr" title="Great Barr">Great Barr</a>, Birmingham</div></div></div>
<p>Many of the friends that Priestley made in Birmingham were members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Society" title="Lunar Society">Lunar Society</a>, a group of manufacturers, inventors, and natural philosophers who assembled monthly to discuss their work. The core of the group included men such as the manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">Matthew Boulton</a>, the chemist and geologist <a href="/wiki/James_Keir" title="James Keir">James Keir</a>, the inventor and engineer <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a>, and the botanist, chemist, and geologist <a href="/wiki/William_Withering" title="William Withering">William Withering</a>. Priestley was asked to join this unique society and contributed much to the work of its members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> As a result of this stimulating intellectual environment, he published several important scientific papers, including "Experiments relating to Phlogiston, and the seeming Conversion of Water into Air" (1783). The first part attempts to refute Lavoisier's challenges to his work on oxygen; the second part describes how steam is "converted" into air. After several variations of the experiment, with different substances as fuel and several different collecting apparatuses (which produced different results), he concluded that air could travel through more substances than previously surmised, a conclusion "contrary to all the known principles of hydrostatics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> This discovery, along with his earlier work on what would later be recognised as gaseous diffusion, would eventually lead <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Graham_(chemist)" title="Thomas Graham (chemist)">Thomas Graham</a> to formulate the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic theory of gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg"><img alt="Full-length portraits of a man and a woman. The man is seated at a table covered with a bright red cloth, looking up at the woman, and she is looking out at the viewer. They occupy the bottom half of the painting, while the top half resembles marble and pillars. She is wearing a white dress with a blue sash and he is wearing a dark coloured suit. Glass chemistry equipment sits on the table and on the floor." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5874" data-file-width="4450" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg/220px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg/330px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg/440px-David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_-_Portrait_of_Monsieur_Lavoisier_and_His_Wife.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> and his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marie-Anne_Pierrette_Paulze" title="Marie-Anne Pierrette Paulze">Marie-Anne Pierrette Paulze</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a>, 1788</div></div></div>
<p>In 1777, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> had written <i>Mémoire sur la combustion en général</i>, the first of what proved to be a series of attacks on <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> it was against these attacks that Priestley responded in 1783. While Priestley accepted parts of Lavoisier's theory, he was unprepared to assent to the major revolutions Lavoisier proposed: the overthrow of phlogiston, a chemistry based conceptually on <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>, and a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry_nomenclature" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry nomenclature">chemical nomenclature</a>. Priestley's original experiments on "dephlogisticated air" (oxygen), combustion, and water provided Lavoisier with the data he needed to construct much of his system; yet Priestley never accepted Lavoisier's new theories and continued to defend phlogiston theory for the rest of his life. Lavoisier's system was based largely on the <i>quantitative</i> concept that <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is neither created nor destroyed in chemical reactions (i.e., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_conservation_of_mass" title="Law of conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>). By contrast, Priestley preferred to observe <i>qualitative</i> changes in heat, color, and particularly volume. His experiments tested "airs" for "their solubility in water, their power of supporting or extinguishing flame, whether they were respirable, how they behaved with acid and alkaline air, and with nitric oxide and inflammable air, and lastly how they were affected by the <a href="/wiki/Electric_spark" title="Electric spark">electric spark</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1789, when Lavoisier published his <i><a href="/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_%C3%89l%C3%A9mentaire_de_Chimie" title="Traité Élémentaire de Chimie">Traité Élémentaire de Chimie</a></i> and founded the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annales_de_Chimie" title="Annales de Chimie">Annales de Chimie</a></i>, the new chemistry had come into its own. Priestley published several more scientific papers in Birmingham, the majority attempting to refute Lavoisier. Priestley and other Lunar Society members argued that the new French system was too expensive, too difficult to test, and unnecessarily complex. Priestley in particular rejected its "establishment" aura. In the end, Lavoisier's view prevailed: his new chemistry introduced many of the principles on which <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">modern chemistry</a> is founded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's refusal to accept Lavoisier's "new chemistry"—such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_conservation_of_mass" title="Law of conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>—and his determination to adhere to a less satisfactory theory has perplexed many scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Schofield explains it thus: "Priestley was never a chemist; in a modern, and even a Lavoisierian, sense, he was never a scientist. He was a natural philosopher, concerned with the economy of nature and obsessed with an idea of unity, in theology and in nature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Historian of science John McEvoy largely agrees, writing that Priestley's view of nature as coextensive with God and thus infinite, which encouraged him to focus on facts over hypotheses and theories, prompted him to reject Lavoisier's system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> McEvoy argues that "Priestley's isolated and lonely opposition to the oxygen theory was a measure of his passionate concern for the principles of intellectual freedom, epistemic equality and critical inquiry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Priestley himself claimed in the last volume of <i>Experiments and Observations</i> that his most valuable works were his theological ones because they were "superior [in] dignity and importance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defender_of_English_Dissenters_and_French_revolutionaries">Defender of English Dissenters and French revolutionaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Defender of English Dissenters and French revolutionaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_and_Dissent" title="Joseph Priestley and Dissent">Joseph Priestley and Dissent</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_Cartoons" title="commons:Joseph Priestley Cartoons">Commons:Joseph Priestley Cartoons</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyCartoon.jpg"><img alt="Caricature of man in frock coat and wig trampling on sacred documents and burning others." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="339" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/PriestleyCartoon.jpg/200px-PriestleyCartoon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/PriestleyCartoon.jpg/300px-PriestleyCartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/PriestleyCartoon.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyCartoon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center><i>DOCTOR PHLOGISTON,<br/> The PRIESTLEY politician or the Political Priest</i></center> This anti-Priestley cartoon shows him trampling on the Bible and burning documents representing English freedom. "Essays on Matter and Spirit", "Gunpowder", and "Revolution Toasts" bulge from his pockets.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Priestley was busy defending phlogiston theory from the "new chemists", most of what he published in Birmingham was theological. In 1782 he published the fourth volume of his <i>Institutes</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_History_of_the_Corruptions_of_Christianity" title="An History of the Corruptions of Christianity">An History of the Corruptions of Christianity</a></i>, describing how he thought the teachings of the early Christian church had been "corrupted" or distorted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Schofield describes the work as "derivative, disorganized, wordy, and repetitive, detailed, exhaustive, and devastatingly argued".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The text addresses issues ranging from the divinity of Christ to the proper form for the Lord's Supper. Priestley followed up in 1786 with the provocatively titled book, <i>An History of Early Opinions concerning Jesus Christ, compiled from Original Writers, proving that the Christian Church was at first Unitarian</i>. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> would later write of the profound effect that these two books had on him: "I have read his Corruptions of Christianity, and Early Opinions of Jesus, over and over again; and I rest on them ... as the basis of my own faith. These writings have never been answered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Although a few readers such as Jefferson and other Rational Dissenters approved of the work, it was harshly reviewed because of its extreme theological positions, particularly its rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1785, while Priestley was engaged in a pamphlet war over <i>Corruptions</i>, he also published <i>The Importance and Extent of Free Enquiry</i>, claiming that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> had not really reformed the church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> In words that would boil over into a national debate, he challenged his readers to enact change:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Let us not, therefore, be discouraged, though, for the present, we should see no great number of churches professedly unitarian .... We are, as it were, laying gunpowder, grain by grain, under the old building of error and superstition, which a single spark may hereafter inflame, so as to produce an instantaneous explosion; in consequence of which that edifice, the erection of which has been the work of ages, may be overturned in a moment, and so effectually as that the same foundation can never be built upon again ....<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although discouraged by friends from using such inflammatory language, Priestley refused to back down from his opinions in print and he included it, forever branding himself as "Gunpowder Joe". After the publication of this seeming call for revolution in the midst of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, pamphleteers stepped up their attacks on Priestley and he and his church were even threatened with legal action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg"><img alt="Caricature of a man preaching out of a barrel labelled &quot;Fanaticism&quot;, stacked up on books labelled &quot;Priestley's works&quot; to a crowd, while the devil sneaks up on him." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2437" data-file-width="3296" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg/220px-A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg/330px-A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg/440px-A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Word_of_Comfort2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"A Word of Comfort" by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_Dent_(cartoonist)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William Dent (cartoonist) (page does not exist)">William Dent</a> (dated 22 March 1790). Priestley is preaching in front of <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_Fox" title="Charles James Fox">Charles James Fox</a> who asks "Pray, Doctor, is there such a thing as a Devil?", to which Priestley responds "No" while the devil prepares to attack Priestley from behind.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1787, 1789, and 1790, Dissenters again tried to repeal the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corporation_Act_1661" title="Corporation Act 1661">Corporation Acts</a>. Although initially it looked as if they might succeed, by 1790, with the fears of revolution looming in Parliament, few were swayed by appeals to equal rights. Political cartoons, one of the most effective and popular media of the time, skewered the Dissenters and Priestley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In Parliament, <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> argued against the repeal, a betrayal that angered Priestley and his friends, who had expected the two men's support. Priestley wrote a series of <i>Letters to William Pitt</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and <i>Letters to Burke</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> in an attempt to persuade them otherwise, but these publications only further inflamed the populace against him.
</p><p>Dissenters such as Priestley who supported the French Revolution came under increasing suspicion as scepticism regarding the revolution grew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In its propaganda against "radicals", Pitt's administration used the "gunpowder" statement to argue that Priestley and other Dissenters wanted to overthrow the government. Burke, in his famous <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_the_Revolution_in_France" title="Reflections on the Revolution in France">Reflections on the Revolution in France</a></i> (1790), tied natural philosophers, and specifically Priestley, to the French Revolution, writing that radicals who supported science in Britain "considered man in their experiments no more than they do mice in an air pump".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Burke also associated <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican</a> principles with alchemy and insubstantial air, mocking the scientific work done by both Priestley and French chemists. He made much in his later writings of the connections between "Gunpowder Joe", science, and Lavoisier—who was improving gunpowder for the French in their <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">war against Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Paradoxically, a secular statesman, Burke, argued against science and maintained that religion should be the basis of civil society, whereas a Dissenting minister, Priestley, argued that religion could not provide the basis for civil society and should be restricted to one's private life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birmingham_riots_of_1791">Birmingham riots of 1791</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Birmingham riots of 1791">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Riots" title="Priestley Riots">Priestley Riots</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg"><img alt="Burning three-story house, surrounded by a mob. People are throwing things out of the windows and belongings are scattered on the street." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="539" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg/220px-Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg/330px-Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg/440px-Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Riots_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The attack on Priestley's home, Fairhill, <a href="/wiki/Sparkbrook" title="Sparkbrook">Sparkbrook</a>, Birmingham</div></div></div>
<p>The animus that had been building against Dissenters and supporters of the American and French Revolutions exploded in July 1791. Priestley and several other Dissenters had arranged to have a celebratory dinner on the anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Storming_of_the_Bastille" title="Storming of the Bastille">storming of the Bastille</a>, a provocative action in a country where many disapproved of the French Revolution and feared that it might spread to Britain. Amid fears of violence, Priestley was convinced by his friends not to attend. Rioters gathered outside the hotel during the banquet and attacked the attendees as they left. The rioters moved on to the New Meeting and Old Meeting churches—and burned both to the ground. Priestley and his wife fled from their home; although their son <a href="/wiki/William_Priestley_(Louisiana_planter)" title="William Priestley (Louisiana planter)">William</a> and others stayed behind to protect their property, the mob overcame them and torched Priestley's house "Fairhill" at <a href="/wiki/Sparkbrook" title="Sparkbrook">Sparkbrook</a>, destroying his valuable laboratory and all of the family's belongings. Twenty-six other Dissenters’ homes and three more churches were burned in the three-day riot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dionisio_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dionisio-138">[138]</a></sup> Priestley spent several days hiding with friends until he was able to travel safely to London. The carefully executed attacks of the "mob" and the farcical trials of only a handful of the "leaders" convinced many at the time—and modern historians later—that the attacks were planned and condoned by local Birmingham <a href="/wiki/Magistrate#United_Kingdom" title="Magistrate">magistrates</a>. When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_III" title="George III">George III</a> was eventually forced to send troops to the area, he said: "I cannot but feel better pleased that Priestley is the sufferer for the doctrines he and his party have instilled, and that the people see them in their true light."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Hackney_.281791.E2.80.9394.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hackney_(1791–94)">Hackney (1791–94)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hackney (1791–94)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style=""><div class="poem">
<p>... Lo! Priestley there, patriot, and saint, and sage,<br/>
Him, full of years, from his loved native land<br/>
Statesmen blood-stained and priests idolatrous<br/>
By dark lies maddening the blind multitude<br/>
Drove with vain hate ....
</p>
</div></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">From "Religious Musings" (1796) by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Unable to return to Birmingham, the Priestleys eventually settled in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Clapton" title="Lower Clapton">Lower Clapton</a>, a district in <a href="/wiki/Hackney_(parish)" title="Hackney (parish)">Hackney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlesex" title="Middlesex">Middlesex</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> where he gave a series of lectures on history and natural philosophy at the <a href="/wiki/Dissenting_academies" title="Dissenting academies">Dissenting academy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_College_at_Hackney" title="New College at Hackney">New College at Hackney</a>. Friends helped the couple rebuild their lives, contributing money, books, and laboratory equipment. Priestley tried to obtain restitution from the government for the destruction of his Birmingham property, but he was never fully reimbursed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> He also published <i>An Appeal to the Public on the Subject of the Riots in Birmingham</i> (1791),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> which indicted the people of Birmingham for allowing the riots to occur and for "violating the principles of English government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1500" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg/220px-Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg/330px-Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg/440px-Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friends_of_the_People_1792_Cruikshank.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Friends of the People</i>, 15 November 1792, caricaturing Joseph Priestley and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Science_History_Institute" title="Science History Institute">Science History Institute</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="516" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg/220px-James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg/330px-James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg/440px-James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Gillray_1793_Dumourier_Dining_in_State_at_St_James_P.933.53.11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dumourier Dining in State at St James's, on 15 May 1793</i> by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a>: Priestley bears a mitre-crowned pie, as he, Fox and Sheridan serve Dumourier<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowden_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-23">[23]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The couple's friends urged them to leave Britain and emigrate to either France or the new United States, even though Priestley had received an appointment to preach for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravel_Pit_Meeting" title="Gravel Pit Meeting">Gravel Pit Meeting</a> congregation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Priestley was minister between 1793 and 1794 and the sermons he preached there, particularly the two <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">Fast</a> Sermons, reflect his growing <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">millenarianism</a>, his belief that the end of the world was fast approaching. After comparing Biblical prophecies to recent history, Priestley concluded that the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> was a harbinger of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming of Christ</a>. Priestley's works had always had a millennial cast, but after the beginning of the French Revolution, this strain increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> He wrote to a younger friend that while he himself would not see the Second Coming, his friend "may probably live to see it ... It cannot, I think be more than twenty years [away]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Daily life became more difficult for the family: Priestley was burned in effigy along with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>; vicious political cartoons continued to be published about him; letters were sent to him from across the country, comparing him to the devil and <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes" title="Guy Fawkes">Guy Fawkes</a>; tradespeople feared the family's business; and Priestley's Royal Academy friends distanced themselves. As the penalties became harsher for those who spoke out against the government, Priestley examined options for removing himself and his family from England.
</p><p>Joseph Priestley's son William was presented to the French Assembly and granted letters of naturalization on 8 June 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Priestley learned about it from the <i>Morning Chronicle</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> A decree of 26 August 1792 by the French National Assembly conferred French citizenship on Joseph Priestley and others who had "served the cause of liberty" by their writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Priestley accepted French citizenship, considering it "the greatest of honours".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>  In the <a href="/wiki/French_National_Convention_election,_1792" title="French National Convention election, 1792">French National Convention election</a> on 5 September 1792, Joseph Priestley was elected to the French <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> by at least two departments, (<a href="/wiki/Orne" title="Orne">Orne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne-et-Loire" title="Rhône-et-Loire">Rhône-et-Loire</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> However, he declined the honour, on the grounds that he was not fluent in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>As relations between England and France worsened, however, a removal to France became impracticable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Following the declaration of war of February 1793, and the Aliens Bill of March 1793, which forbade correspondence or travel between England and France, William Priestley left France for America. Joseph Priestley's sons Harry and Joseph chose to leave England for America in August 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Finally Priestley himself followed with his wife, boarding the <i>Sansom</i> at Gravesend on 7 April 1794.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Five weeks after Priestley left, <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt's</a> administration began arresting radicals for <a href="/wiki/Seditious_libel" title="Seditious libel">seditious libel</a>, resulting in the famous <a href="/wiki/1794_Treason_Trials" title="1794 Treason Trials">1794 Treason Trials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Pennsylvania_.281794.E2.80.931804.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pennsylvania_(1794–1804)">Pennsylvania (1794–1804)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pennsylvania (1794–1804)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyPeale.jpg"><img alt="Half-length portrait of an elderly man with wispy brown hair. He is wearing a black jacket and a white shirt." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="691" data-file-width="504" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/PriestleyPeale.jpg/170px-PriestleyPeale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/PriestleyPeale.jpg/255px-PriestleyPeale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/PriestleyPeale.jpg/340px-PriestleyPeale.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PriestleyPeale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley, painted late in life by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Peale" title="Rembrandt Peale">Rembrandt Peale</a> (c. 1800);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Americans knew Priestley less as a man of science and more as a defender of the freedom of the colonies and of Dissenters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_House" title="Joseph Priestley House">Joseph Priestley House</a></div>
<p>The Priestleys arrived in New York City on 4 June 1794, where they were <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/f%C3%AAte" title="wikt:fête">fêted</a> by various political factions vying for Priestley's endorsement. Priestley declined their entreaties, hoping to avoid political discord in his new country. Before travelling to a new home in the backwoods of <a href="/wiki/Northumberland_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Northumberland County, Pennsylvania">Northumberland County, Pennsylvania</a>, at Point township (now the <a href="/wiki/Northumberland,_Pennsylvania" title="Northumberland, Pennsylvania">Borough of Northumberland</a>), Dr and Mrs Priestley lodged in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, where Priestley gave a series of sermons which led to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/First_Unitarian_Church_of_Philadelphia" title="First Unitarian Church of Philadelphia">First Unitarian Church of Philadelphia</a>. Priestley turned down an opportunity to teach chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's son Joseph Priestley Jr. was a leading member of a consortium that had purchased 300,000 acres of virgin woodland between the forks of <a href="/wiki/Loyalsock_Creek" title="Loyalsock Creek">Loyalsock Creek</a>, which they intended to lease or sell in 400-acre plots, with payment deferred to seven annual instalments, with interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> His brothers, <a href="/wiki/William_Priestley_(Louisiana_planter)" title="William Priestley (Louisiana planter)">William</a> and Henry, bought a 284-acre plot of woodland which they attempted to transform into a farm, later called "Fairhill", felling and uprooting trees, and making <a href="/wiki/Liming_(soil)" title="Liming (soil)">lime</a> to sweeten the soil by building their own lime kilns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Henry Priestley died 11 December 1795, possibly of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> which he may have contracted after landing at New York. Mary Priestley's health, already poor, deteriorated further; although <a href="/wiki/William_Priestley_(Louisiana_planter)" title="William Priestley (Louisiana planter)">William's</a> wife, Margaret Foulke-Priestley, moved in with the couple to nurse Mary twenty-four hours a day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Mary Priestley died 17 September 1796.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Dr Priestley now moved in with his elder son, Joseph Jr., and his wife Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cooper_(U.S._politician)" title="Thomas Cooper (U.S. politician)">Thomas Cooper</a>, whose son, Thomas Jr., was living with the Priestleys, was a frequent visitor.
</p><p>Since his arrival in America, Priestley had continued to defend his <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Christian Unitarian</a> beliefs; now, falling increasingly under the influence of Thomas Cooper and Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley, he was unable to avoid becoming embroiled in political controversy. In 1798, when, in response to the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Pinckney affair</a>, a belligerent <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">President Adams</a> sought to enlarge the navy and mobilise the militia into what Priestley and Cooper saw as a 'standing army', Priestley published an anonymous newspaper article: <i>Maxims of political arithmetic</i>, which attacked Adams, defended free trade, and advocated a form of Jeffersonian isolationism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In the same year, a small package, addressed vaguely: "Dr Priestley in America," was seized by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> on board a neutral Danish boat. It was found to contain three letters, one of which was signed by the radical printer <a href="/wiki/John_Hurford_Stone" title="John Hurford Stone">John Hurford Stone</a>. These intercepted letters were published in London, and copied in numerous papers in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> One of the letters was addressed to "MBP", with a note: "I inclose a note for our friend MBP—but, as ignorant of the name he bears at present among you, I must beg you to seal and address it." This gave the intercepted letters a tinge of intrigue. Fearful lest they be taken as evidence of him being a 'spy in the interest of France', Priestley sent a clumsy letter to numerous newspaper editors, in which he naively named "MBP" (Member of the British Parliament) as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Vaughan" title="Benjamin Vaughan">Mr. Benjamin Vaughan</a>, who "like me, thought it necessary to leave England, and for some time is said to have assumed a feigned name."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Cobbett" title="William Cobbett">William Cobbett</a>, in his <i>Porcupine's Gazette</i>, 20 August 1798, added that Priestley "has told us who Mr MBP is, and has confirmed me in the opinion of their both being spies in the interest of France."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of a two-story, white clapboard house" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2022" data-file-width="3410" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg/220px-Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg/330px-Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg/440px-Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_House_Front_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Priestleys' rural Pennsylvania home never became the center of a <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> community, as the expected emigrants could not afford the journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Joseph Priestley Jr. left on a visit to England at Christmas 1798, not returning until August 1800. In his absence, his wife Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley and Thomas Cooper became increasing close, collaborating in numerous political essays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Priestley allowed himself to fall too heavily under Elizabeth and Cooper's influences, even helping hawk a seditious handbill Cooper had printed, around <a href="/wiki/Northumberland,_Pennsylvania" title="Northumberland, Pennsylvania">Point township</a>, and across the <a href="/wiki/Susquehanna_River" title="Susquehanna River">Susquehanna</a> at <a href="/wiki/Sunbury,_Pennsylvania" title="Sunbury, Pennsylvania">Sunbury</a>. In September 1799, <a href="/wiki/William_Cobbett" title="William Cobbett">William Cobbett</a> printed extracts from this handbill, asserting that: "Dr Priestley has taken great pains to circulate this address, has travelled through the country for the purpose, and is in fact the patron of it." He challenged Priestley to "clear himself of the accusation" or face prosecution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Barely a month later, in November and December 1799, Priestley stepped forward in his own defence, with his <i>Letters to the inhabitants of Northumberland</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg/220px-Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg/330px-Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg/440px-Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Priestley_Graves_VIII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Priestley's original 1804 gravestone in Riverview Cemetery, Northumberland, Pennsylvania. Visible at right is part of the new stone, placed in front of it in 1971.</div></div></div>
<p>Priestley’s son, <a href="/wiki/William_Priestley_(Louisiana_planter)" title="William Priestley (Louisiana planter)">William</a>, now living in Philadelphia, was increasingly embarrassed by his father's actions. He confronted his father, expressing <a href="/wiki/John_Vaughan_(wine_merchant)" title="John Vaughan (wine merchant)">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Vaughan" title="Benjamin Vaughan">Benjamin Vaughan</a>’s unease, his own wife's concerns about Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley's dietary care,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> and his own concerns at the closeness of Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley and Thomas Cooper's relationship, and their adverse influence on Dr Priestley; but this only led to a further estrangement between William and his sister-in-law. When, a while later, Priestley's household suffered a bout of food poisoning, perhaps from <a href="/wiki/Milk_sickness" title="Milk sickness">milk sickness</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic_bacteria" title="Pathogenic bacteria">bacterial infection</a>, Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley, falsely accused William of having poisoned the family's flour. Although this allegation has attracted the attention of some modern historians, it is believed to be without foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley continued the educational projects that had always been important to him, helping to establish the "Northumberland Academy" and donating his library to the fledgling institution. He exchanged letters regarding the proper structure of a university with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, who used this advice when founding the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>. Jefferson and Priestley became close, and when he had completed his <i>General History of the Christian Church</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> he dedicated it to President Jefferson, writing that "it is now only that I can say I see nothing to fear from the hand of power, the government under which I live being for the first time truly favourable to me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley tried to continue his scientific investigations in America with the support of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>. He was hampered by lack of news from Europe; unaware of the latest scientific developments, Priestley was no longer on the forefront of discovery. Although the majority of his publications focused on defending <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a>, he also did some original work on <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a> and dreams. Despite Priestley's reduced scientific output, his presence stimulated American interest in chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1801, Priestley had become so ill that he could no longer write or experiment. He died on the morning of 6 February 1804,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> aged seventy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> and was buried at Riverview Cemetery in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland,_Pennsylvania" title="Northumberland, Pennsylvania">Northumberland, Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestley's epitaph reads:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>Return unto thy rest, O my soul, for the<br/>
Lord hath dealt bountifully with thee.<br/>
I will lay me down in peace and sleep till<br/>
I awake in the morning of the resurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Priestley&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:353px;max-width:353px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:147px;max-width:147px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1528" data-file-width="829" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg/145px-Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg/218px-Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg/290px-Chamberlain_Square_Statue_Priestley.jpg 2x" width="145"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>Statue of Priestley by <a href="/wiki/Francis_John_Williamson" title="Francis John Williamson">Francis John Williamson</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Chamberlain_Square" title="Chamberlain Square">Chamberlain Square</a>, Birmingham, England.</center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Priestley_(3006516470).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg/200px-Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg/300px-Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg/400px-Joseph_Priestley_%283006516470%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><center>Statue of Priestly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_Square,_Leeds" title="City Square, Leeds">City Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a></center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>By the time he died in 1804, Priestley had been made a member of every major scientific society in the Western world and he had discovered numerous substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The 19th-century French naturalist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Cuvier" title="George Cuvier">George Cuvier</a>, in his eulogy of Priestley, praised his discoveries while at the same time lamenting his refusal to abandon phlogiston theory, calling him "the father of modern chemistry [who] never acknowledged his daughter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Priestley published more than 150 works on topics ranging from political philosophy to education to theology to natural philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> He led and inspired British radicals during the 1790s, paved the way for <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tapper,_322_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tapper,_322-185">[185]</a></sup> and helped found <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> A wide variety of philosophers, scientists, and poets became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_Ideas" title="Association of Ideas">associationists</a> as a result of his <a href="/wiki/Redaction" title="Redaction">redaction</a> of David Hartley's <i><a href="/wiki/Observations_on_Man" title="Observations on Man">Observations on Man</a></i>, including <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Darwin" title="Erasmus Darwin">Erasmus Darwin</a>, Coleridge, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Bain" title="Alexander Bain">Alexander Bain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> praised Priestley in his <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Pure_Reason" title="Critique of Pure Reason">Critique of Pure Reason</a></i> (1781), writing that he "knew how to combine his paradoxical teaching with the interests of religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tap314_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tap314-6">[6]</a></sup> Indeed, it was Priestley's aim to "put the most 'advanced' Enlightenment ideas into the service of a rationalized though heterodox Christianity, under the guidance of the basic principles of scientific method".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tapper,_322_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tapper,_322-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Considering the extent of Priestley's influence, relatively little scholarship has been devoted to him. In the early 20th century, Priestley was most often described as a conservative and dogmatic scientist who was nevertheless a political and religious reformer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> In a historiographic review essay, historian of science <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schaffer" title="Simon Schaffer">Simon Schaffer</a> describes the two dominant portraits of Priestley: the first depicts him as "a playful innocent" who stumbled across his discoveries; the second portrays him as innocent as well as "warped" for not understanding their implications better. Assessing Priestley's works as a whole has been difficult for scholars because of his wide-ranging interests. His scientific discoveries have usually been divorced from his theological and metaphysical publications to make an analysis of his life and writings easier, but this approach has been challenged recently by scholars such as John McEvoy and Robert Schofield. Although early Priestley scholarship claimed that his theological and metaphysical works were "distractions" and "obstacles" to his scientific work, scholarship published in the 1960s, 1970s, and 1980s maintained that Priestley's works constituted a unified theory. However, as Schaffer explains, no convincing synthesis of his work has yet been expounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> More recently, in 2001, historian of science Dan Eshet has argued that efforts to create a "synoptic view" have resulted only in a rationalisation of the contradictions in Priestley's thought, because they have been "organized around philosophical categories" and have "separate[d] the producers of scientific ideas from any social conflict".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="993" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png/220px-Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png/330px-Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png/440px-Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_plaque_-_Joseph_Priestley_-_New_Meeting_Street_Birmingham_-_Andy_Mabbett.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Royal Society of Chemistry">Royal Society of Chemistry</a> commemorates Priestley at New Meeting Street, Birmingham</div></div></div>
<p>Priestley has been remembered by the towns in which he served as a reforming educator and minister and by the scientific organisations he influenced. Two educational institutions have been named in his honour—<a href="/wiki/Priestley_College" title="Priestley College">Priestley College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley_College" title="Joseph Priestley College">Joseph Priestley College</a> in <a href="/wiki/City_of_Leeds" title="City of Leeds">Leeds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> (now part of <a href="/wiki/Leeds_City_College" title="Leeds City College">Leeds City College</a>)—and an asteroid, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5577_Priestley" title="5577 Priestley">5577 Priestley</a>, discovered in 1986 by <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Waldron" title="Duncan Waldron">Duncan Waldron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Birstall,_West_Yorkshire" title="Birstall, West Yorkshire">Birstall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Leeds_City_Square" title="Leeds City Square">Leeds City Square</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Chamberlain_Square" title="Chamberlain Square">Birmingham</a>, he is memorialised through statues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and plaques commemorating him have been posted in Birmingham, <a href="/wiki/Calne" title="Calne">Calne</a> and Warrington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Also, since 1952 <a href="/wiki/Dickinson_College" title="Dickinson College">Dickinson College</a>, Pennsylvania, has presented the Priestley Award to a scientist who makes "discoveries which contribute to the welfare of mankind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> The main undergraduate chemistry laboratories at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leeds" title="University of Leeds">University of Leeds</a> were refurbished as part of a £4m refurbishment plan in 2006 and renamed as the Priestley Laboratories in his honour as a prominent chemist from Leeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> In 2016 the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Huddersfield" title="University of Huddersfield">University of Huddersfield</a> renamed the building housing its Applied Sciences department as the Joseph Priestley Building, as part of an exercise to rename all campus buildings after prominent local figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additional recognition for Priestley's work is marked by a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Chemical_Landmarks" title="National Historic Chemical Landmarks">National Historic Chemical Landmark</a> designation for his discovery of oxygen, made on 1 August 1994, at the Priestley House in Northumberland, Penn., by the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a>. Similar international recognition was made on 7 August 2000, at <a href="/wiki/Bowood_House" title="Bowood House">Bowood House</a> in Wiltshire, UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> The ACS also awards their highest honor, the <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Medal" title="Priestley Medal">Priestley Medal</a>, in his name.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other early investigators who suspected that the electrical force diminished with distance as the gravitational force did (i.e., as the inverse square of the distance) included <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli" title="Daniel Bernoulli">Daniel Bernoulli</a> (see: Abel Socin (1760)  <i>Acta Helvetia</i>, vol. 4, pp. 224–25.) and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a>, both of whom measured the force between plates of a capacitor, and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Aepinus" title="Franz Aepinus">Aepinus</a>. See: J.L. Heilbron, <i>Electricity in the 17th and 18th Centuries: A Study of Early Modern Physics</i> (Los Angeles, California: University of California Press, 1979), pp. 460–62,  <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=UlTLRUn1sy8C&amp;pg=PA464#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">464</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160514033716/https://books.google.com/books?id=UlTLRUn1sy8C&amp;pg=PA464" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 14 May 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. (including footnote 44).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Joseph Priestley, <i>The History and Present State of Electricity, with Original Experiments</i> (London, England: 1767), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=HZE_AAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA732#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 732</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160528211919/https://books.google.com/books?id=HZE_AAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA732" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 28 May 2016 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.:<br/><blockquote><p>May we not infer from this experiment, that the attraction of electricity is subject to the same laws with that of gravitation, and is therefore according to the squares of the distances; since it is easily demonstrated, that were the earth in the form of a shell, a body in the inside of it would not be attracted to one side more than another?</p></blockquote></span>

<li id="cite_note-167"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-167">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vaughan had fled to France in May 1794, when <a href="/wiki/John_Hurford_Stone" title="John Hurford Stone">John Hurford Stone</a>'s brother, William, was arrested and found to have a letter from Vaughan. <li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In December 1799, two of Elizabeth Ryland-Priestley's essays, <i>On the propriety and expediency of unlimited enquiry</i>, and <i>A Reply to</i> [Thomas Cooper's] <i>Observations on the Fast Day</i> [Cooper had challenged the power of a President to declare a day of fasting and prayer], were published as part of <i>Political essays</i> (Northumberland, PA, 1799). <li id="cite_note-194"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-194">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Society_Moonstones" title="Lunar Society Moonstones">Lunar Society Moonstones</a> honour Priestley in Birmingham. There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Plaque" title="Blue Plaque">Blue Plaques</a> commemorating him on the side of the Church of St. Michael and St. Joseph, New Meeting House Lane, Birmingham (<a class="external text" href="http://www.birminghamcs.org.uk/blueplaques.htm" rel="nofollow">Birmingham Civic Society</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080210082556/http://www.birminghamcs.org.uk/blueplaques.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 10 February 2008 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. Retrieved 1 January 2010), and another on the Warrington Salvation Army Citadel, once the home of Priestley (<a class="external text" href="http://img.cryst.bbk.ac.uk/BCA/CNews/1998/Sep98/PLinfo.html" rel="nofollow">British Crystallographic Association</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060925103051/http://img.cryst.bbk.ac.uk/BCA/CNews/1998/Sep98/PLinfo.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 25 September 2006 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. Retrieved 1 January 2010).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-195"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-195">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.dickinson.edu/news/priestley/index.html" rel="nofollow">Joseph Priestley Celebration</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071101074841/http://www.dickinson.edu/news/priestley/index.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 1 November 2007 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.. <a href="/wiki/Dickinson_College" title="Dickinson College">Dickinson College</a>. Retrieved 1 January 2010.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://reporter.leeds.ac.uk/press_releases/current/assets/sainsbury1.pdf" rel="nofollow">The official opening of the Priestley Laboratories by Lord Sainsbury</a>, 20 October 2006, University of Leeds, Retrieved 1 January 2010.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-197"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-197">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170105084041/https://www.hud.ac.uk/news/2016/april/buildingnamechanges.php" rel="nofollow">"University of Huddersfield"</a>. <i>www.hud.ac.uk</i>.
